Resumen 
Tessellum es una aplicación de escritorio de prioridad local ( local-first) para la gestión del 
conocimiento personal, cuyo propósito es facilitar la creación, organización, recuperación y 
exploración de información personal almacenada en forma de notas Markdown. El proyecto 
aborda el problema de la fragmentación de la informaci ón, entendida como la dispersión del 
conocimiento útil en documentos desconectados que resultan difíciles de estructurar, relacionar 
y revisar de manera eficiente. Para mitigar este problema, la aplicación proporciona mecanismos 
como el enlace bidireccional, la organización basada en etiquetas, la búsqueda de texto 
completo y la visualización en grafo de las relaciones entre notas, permitiendo a los usuarios 
navegar por su información de una manera más coherente y contextualizada. 
Desde una perspectiva técnica, Tessellum se ha implementado como una aplicación de escritorio 
basada en Tauri, con un frontend en React y TypeScript y un backend en Rust. Su arquitectura 
local-first garantiza que el contenido del usuario permanezca almacenado en carpetas locales 
seleccionadas por el propio usuario, siendo los archivos Markdown la fuente de verdad primaria; 
de este modo, se preserva la propiedad de los datos, su portabilidad y la inde pendencia de los 
servicios en la nube. Además, el sistema incorpora indexación local y estructuras de datos 
derivadas para dar soporte a búsquedas eficientes, extracción de metadatos, análisis de 
relaciones y consultas sobre el grafo sin alterar los archivos originales del usuario. 
En su estado actual, Tessellum debe entenderse como un proyecto de software independiente 
con aplicabilidad práctica, más que como una solución desarrollada para un cliente externo 
específico. El repositorio refleja un nivel de desarrollo orientado al uso real, incluyendo flujos de 
trabajo de edición y previsualización de notas, soporte para plantillas y notas diarias, 
extensibilidad mediante complementos ( plugins), personalización de la accesibilidad y la 
apariencia, herramientas de exploración del grafo y  materiales para la evaluación de la 
usabilidad. En consecuencia, el proyecto puede caracterizarse como una aplicación de gestión 
del conocimiento funcional y extensible, diseñada para reducir la fragmentación de la 
información manteniendo un entorno de trabajo local, portátil y controlado por el usuario. 
 
Palabras Clave 
Bóveda, Gestión del Conocimiento Personal (GCP), Fragmentación de la Información, Grafo de 
Conocimiento, Arquitectura de Prioridad Local, Enlaces bidireccionales. 
 
Abstract

Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo | Jorge Carriles Ruiz 5 
 
Tessellum is a local -first desktop application for personal knowledge management whose 
purpose is to facilitate the creation, organization, retrieval, and exploration of personal 
information stored as Markdown notes. The project addresses the problem of in formation 
fragmentation, understood as the dispersion of useful knowledge across disconnected 
documents that are difficult to structure, relate, and revisit efficiently. To mitigate this issue, the 
application provides mechanisms such as bi-directional linking, tag-based organization, full-text 
search, and graph -based visualization of note relationships, allowing users to navigate their 
information in a more coherent and contextualized manner. 
From a technical perspective, Tessellum has been implemented as a desktop application based 
on Tauri, with a React and TypeScript frontend and a Rust backend. Its local -first architecture 
ensures that user content remains stored in regular folders selected by the user, with Markdown 
files as the primary source of truth, thereby preserving data ownership, portability, and 
independence from cloud services. In addition, the system incorporates local indexing and 
derived data structures to support efficient sea rch, metadata extraction, relationship analysis, 
and graph queries without altering the original user’s files. 
In its current state, Tessellum should be understood as an independent software project with 
practical applicability rather than as a solution developed for a specific external client. The 
repository reflects a level of development oriented towards real us e, including editable and 
preview-based note workflows, template and daily -note support, plugin -based extensibility, 
accessibility and appearance customization, graph exploration tools, and materials for usability 
evaluation. Consequently, the project can be characterized as a functional and extensible 
knowledge management application designed to reduce information fragmentation while 
maintaining a local, portable, and user-controlled working environment. 
Keywords 
Vault, Personal Knowledge Management (PKM), Information Fragmentation, Knowledge Graph, 
Local-First Architecture, Bi-directional Linking

1 Presentation 
The purpose of this presentation is to provide an initial framework for the documentation of 
the Tessellum project, justifying the organization of the material and guiding the reader 
through the structure that will be followed in the subsequent pages. In a project developed 
within the field of software engineering, the result cannot be understood only through the 
description of the implemented product. It must also be supported by a clear exposition of the 
ideas, concepts, and criteria that give meaning to the decisions taken throughout the work. For 
that reason, this opening text serves as a brief but necessary introduction, intended to orient 
the reader before entering into the more detailed parts of the document. 
 
In line with the academic and technical spirit associated with UNE 157801, this presentation 
does not aim to discuss the project in depth, nor to anticipate the full content of the report. Its 
function is instead to establish the context of reading, explain the logic behind the 
documentary sequence, and make explicit the route through which the reader will 
progressively approach the project. This introductory role is especially relevant in technical 
documentation, where clarity of structure is essential to ensure that the different parts of the 
work are interpreted in a coherent and ordered way. 
 
The development of Tessellum responds to a process in which theoretical understanding and 
practical implementation are closely linked. Consequently, the document has been organized 
so that the reader can first become familiar with the conceptual and methodological 
foundations that justify the project, and only afterwards move on to the detailed explanation 
of the work carried out. This progression is not merely formal. It is intended to ensure that the 
later description of the design, construction, and implementation of the software is read with 
the appropriate prior context, allowing the technical decisions adopted during the 
development process to be understood more fully. 
 
Accordingly, the following sections will first introduce the theoretical aspects that frame the 
work. These include the principles, technologies, and general considerations that are necessary 
to understand the nature of the problem addressed and the rationale behind the proposed 
solution. Once this theoretical foundation has been established, the document will focus more 
directly on the project report itself, where the development process, the main design choices, 
the implementation work, and the resulting characteristics of Tessellum will be presented in a 
concrete and structured manner. 
 
This arrangement seeks to guide the reader from the general to the specific, from the 
conceptual basis of the project to its practical materialization as a software product. In this 
way, the presentation fulfills a double purpose: on the one hand, it justifies the organization of 
the document; on the other, it provides the reader with the orientation needed to approach 
the subsequent sections with a clear understanding of their role within the overall report. The 
result is a more coherent and accessible reading path, consistent with the objective of 
documenting the project in a rigorous, academic, and technically well-founded manner.

2 Theoretical Aspects 
This section outlines the fundamental concepts, technologies, and tools utilized in the 
development of Project Tessellum. The objective is to provide a concise overview of each 
element—detailing its origin, industry relevance, and specific application with in this project —
rather than an exhaustive technical tutorial. Readers seeking more in -depth characteristics or 
documentation are encouraged to consult the external resources, web links, and literature 
referenced in the project bibliography. 
Special emphasis has been placed on detailing the most novel elements of the system's 
architecture. The content is structured into three main areas: 
• Concepts: Explores the theoretical paradigms and methodologies driving the project. 
This includes foundational models such as Local-First Software, Knowledge Graphs, and 
Bi-directional Linking, as well as the Scrum framework used to manage the development 
cycle. 
• Technologies: Details the core programming languages, frameworks, and libraries that 
compose Tessellum's architecture. It covers both the high -performance backend stack 
(Rust, Tauri, SQLite, Tantivy) and the reactive frontend ecosystem (React, TypeScript, 
Cytoscape.js, CodeMirror 6), specifying their origins, purposes, and the exact versions 
employed. 
• Tools: Describes the development environments, package managers, version control 
systems, and planning software (e.g., Vite, Git, RustRover, Microsoft Project) that 
facilitated the engineering, testing, and management phases of the project. 
 
2.1 Concepts 
2.1.1 Local-First Software 
2.1.1.1 Origin and Definition  
The concept of "Local-first software" was formally defined in 2019 by researchers at the Ink & 
Switch industrial research lab (Kleppmann, Wiggins, van Hardenberg, and McGranaghan). It 
emerged as a response to the growing dependency on "Cloud-first" applications, which prioritize 
remote servers for data storage and logic. Local -first software is built upon  seven core ideals: 
fast response, multi-device synchronization, offline capability, collaboration, longevity, privacy, 
and user control. 
Commented [JC4]: @ENRIQUE JUAN DE ANDRES GALIANA 
Sección para revisar 
===== PAGE 15 =====
Project Memory | Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs 
Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo | Jorge Carriles Ruiz 15 
 
2.1.1.2 Working Method and Importance  
In a traditional cloud application, the user's data depends on a service provider's infrastructure; 
if the server goes down or the company shuts down, the data becomes inaccessible. Local -first 
software changes this by making the local device the primary source of truth. Data is stored on 
the user's own hardware in open or standard formats. 
This paradigm is relevant mainly because of data sovereignty. By removing the need for a 
constant internet connection and a central authority, the software achieves low latency — since 
there is no network round -trip involved — and ensures that the user's n otes and knowledge 
remain accessible over time, regardless of the state of external service providers. 
2.1.1.3 Application in Project Tessellum  
In the context of Tessellum, the local -first concept is the foundational architectural pillar. We 
use it for the following purposes: 
• The "Vault" Concept: Tessellum does not hide data in a proprietary database. Instead, 
it treats a standard folder on the user’s filesystem as the "Vault." This ensures the user 
can open their notes with any other Markdown editor at any time. 
• Performance: By indexing files locally using Rust and Tantivy, we achieve near -
instantaneous search and graph rendering results that would be impossible to maintain 
over a standard web API. 
• Privacy by Design:  Since the project's scope excludes cloud synchronization, no user 
data ever leaves the machine. This makes Tessellum suitable for "Technical Users" who 
handle sensitive information or proprietary documentation. 
By adhering to this concept, Tessellum achieves a level of "software permanence" where the 
application serves the data, rather than the data being a product of the application. 
 
2.1.2 Knowledge Graphs and Bi-directional Linking 
2.1.2.1 Origin and Definition  
The concept of the "Knowledge Graph" was popularized by Google in 2012 to describe its 
semantic search capabilities, but its academic roots lie in Graph Theory (pioneered by Leonhard 
Euler) and the Semantic Web. In the context of personal knowledge management (PKM), it 
refers to an information model where data is stored as a network of nodes (concepts or notes) 
and edges (the relationships between them). Bi-directional linking  is the mechanism that 
facilitates this graph; it ensures that if Note A links to Note B, Note B automatically "knows" it is 
being referenced by Note A (a backlink). 
===== PAGE 16 =====
Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs | Project Memory 
16 Jorge Carriles Ruiz | Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo 
 
2.1.2.2 Working Method and Importance  
Unlike traditional file systems that rely on a hierarchical structure (folders and subfolders), a 
Knowledge Graph allows for a flat or networked structure . In a hierarchy, a file can typically 
only exist in one "place" at a time. In a graph, a note can be connected to an infinite number of 
other notes regardless of their "location" on the disk. 
The importance of this method lies in its alignment with human cognition. The human brain 
works through association, not categorization. By using bi-directional links, a system allows for 
the emergence of patterns : as the number of connections grows, the user discovers non -
obvious relationships between disparate ideas, turning a simple collection of notes into a 
cohesive "Second Brain." 
2.1.2.3 Application in Project Tessellum  
Within Tessellum, these concepts are implemented to turn static Markdown files into a more 
structured knowledge tool: 
• Backlink discovery: The Rust backend scans the vault to identify every instance where a 
file is referenced. This information is displayed in the UI, allowing the user to see the 
context of each reference to a given idea. 
• The Grafeo engine:  This is the part of the project responsible for translating the 
relational data stored in the SQLx cache into a graph -compatible format. It calculates 
node weights — based on link density — and edge directions. 
• Interactive visualization:  Using Cytoscape.js, Tessellum includes a graph view that 
functions as a navigational tool. The user can explore their knowledge base visually, 
identifying isolated notes or densely connected clusters of information. 
By implementing a knowledge graph, Tessellum extends its functionality beyond a text editor, 
offering a way to analyze the structure of the stored information and the relationships between 
ideas. 
 
2.1.3 Asynchronous Programming 
2.1.3.1 Origin and Definition  
Asynchronous programming is a design pattern that allows a computer program to initiate a task 
and move on to another task before the first one has finished. Unlike traditional Synchronous 
(Blocking) programming—where the execution thread must wait for an operation (like reading 
a file or a network request) to complete —asynchronous execution enables concurrency by 
"suspending" tasks that are waiting for I/O and utilizing the CPU for other work in the meantime. 
While the roots of this concept date back to ea rly cooperative multitasking, it has reached 
===== PAGE 17 =====
Project Memory | Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs 
Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo | Jorge Carriles Ruiz 17 
 
modern maturity through event -loop architectures and the "async/await" syntax found in 
languages like Rust, JavaScript, and C#. 
2.1.3.2 Working Method and Importance  
The primary mechanism behind asynchronous programming is the Event Loop  or a Task 
Scheduler. Instead of creating a new OS thread for every single task (which is resource-intensive 
and leads to high memory overhead), the program creates "lightweight tasks" or "Green 
threads." When a task reaches a point where it must wait for the disk or the netw ork, it yields 
control back to the scheduler, which picks up the next ready task. 
The importance of this approach is two-fold: 
1. Responsiveness: It prevents the "Application Not Responding" (UI freeze) state by 
ensuring the main thread is never blocked by heavy computations or slow I/O. 
2. Scalability: It allows the application to handle thousands of concurrent operations —
such as monitoring a large "Vault" of files—with a minimal memory footprint. 
2.1.3.3 Application in Project Tessellum  
In Tessellum, asynchronous programming is not simply an optional feature, but a core 
requirement for the local-first experience. It is implemented using the Tokio runtime in Rust and 
the asynchronous IPC (Inter-Process Communication) bridge provided by Tauri: 
• Non-blocking indexing:  When a user opens a vault containing a large number of 
Markdown files, the system starts a background indexing task. Since this operation is 
asynchronous, the user can begin typing or searching immediately while the indexer 
populates the database in the background. 
• Real-time file watching:  The Notify crate operates asynchronously, listening for 
filesystem events. When a file is saved, the update to the Tantivy search index is handled 
by a background worker, avoiding interruptions in the text editor. 
• Search query execution:  Search results are fetched asynchronously. The UI sends a 
search command and continues rendering or handling user input while the Rust backend 
processes the query. 
This asynchronous architecture allows Tessellum to remain responsive under significant data 
loads, which is one of the practical advantages of building on Rust's concurrency model.

Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs | Project Memory 
18 Jorge Carriles Ruiz | Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo 
 
2.1.4 Scrum Methodology 
2.1.4.1 Origin and Definition  
Scrum is an Agile framework designed to manage complex product development. While its 
conceptual roots can be traced to a 1986 Harvard Business Review  article by Takeuchi and 
Nonaka, it was formally codified and presented by Jeff Sutherland and Ken Schwaber in 1995. 
Unlike traditional "Waterfall" methodologies that rely on rigid, upfront planning, Scrum is built 
on empiricism: the idea that knowledge comes from experience and making decisions based on 
what is observed. 
2.1.4.2 Working Method and Importance  
The methodology is structured around three pillars: transparency, inspection, and adaptation. 
Work is organized into fixed -length iterations called Sprints (usually 1 to 4 weeks). Each Sprint 
begins with a planning session and ends with a review and retrospective to evaluate the product 
and the process. The framework defines three specific roles (Product Owner, Scrum Master, and 
Developers) and three artifacts (Product Backlog, Sprint Backlog, and the Increment). 
The importance of Scrum in modern software engineering lies in its ability to handle uncertainty. 
By delivering small, functional increments of software frequently, teams can pivot based on 
feedback, reduce the risk of large-scale failure, and ensure that the most valuable features are 
developed first. 
2.1.4.3 Application in Project Tessellum  
For Project Tessellum, Scrum serves as the primary management engine to ensure the project 
meets the academic deadline while maintaining high technical quality: 
• Hybrid Scrum Role: Although the execution is centralized in a single individual (as noted 
in the OBS), the project is managed by alternating between roles: acting as the Product 
Owner when prioritizing the 40+ user stories, and as the Developer during the coding 
phase. 
• Backlog Management: All project requirements were translated into a Product Backlog 
of User Stories. This allowed for clear visibility of the "Definition of Done" (DoD) for 
complex features like the Grafeo engine or the Tantivy search integration. 
• Iterative Development: The project was divided into Sprints, each focused on a specific 
"Epic". This ensured that a Minimum Viable Product (MVP) was functional early in the 
development cycle. 
• Adaptability: The use of Scrum allowed for the "Out-of-Scope" boundaries (Section 3.9) 
to be strictly maintained, preventing scope creep when technical challenges arose 
during the implementation of the Rust backend. 
===== PAGE 19 =====
Project Memory | Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs 
Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo | Jorge Carriles Ruiz 19 
 
By applying Scrum, the documentation and development of Tessellum follow a professional, 
industry-standard rhythm that guarantees transparency and milestone validation. 
 
2.1.5 Personal Knowledge Management (PKM) & 
Second Brain 
2.1.5.1 Origin and Definition 
The "Second Brain" is a methodology popularized by Tiago Forte, though its roots lie in the 
"Memex" (Vannevar Bush, 1945). It refers to an external, digital system for capturing, organizing, 
and synthesizing ideas to augment human memory. 
2.1.5.2 Working Method and Importance 
Unlike simple note -taking, a PKM system focuses on thought synthesis. It moves away from 
passive storage toward an active environment where notes "interact" with each other. Its 
importance lies in solving the "information overload" of the modern era, turning raw data into 
actionable knowledge. 
2.1.5.3 Application in Project Tessellum 
This is the central idea behind the project. Tessellum is designed not only to store text, but to 
support the user's thinking process. By combining the knowledge graph with a distraction -free 
writing environment, the application reduces the need to manuall y track where information is 
stored, allowing the user to focus on establishing connections between ideas. 
 
2.1.6 Contextual Command Patterns (Slash 
Commands) 
2.1.6.1 Origin and Definition 
Popularized by modern productivity tools like Notion and Slack, "Slash Commands" are a UX 
pattern where typing a forward slash (/) triggers a contextual menu of formatting options and 
components. 
2.1.6.2 Working Method and Importance 
This pattern implements "Interfaceless Design" principles. Its importance lies in flow-state 
preservation: it allows power users to perform complex formatting, insert components, or 
===== PAGE 20 =====
Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs | Project Memory 
20 Jorge Carriles Ruiz | Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo 
 
trigger logic without ever lifting their hands from the keyboard or navigating complex nested 
menus. 
2.1.6.3 Application in Project Tessellum 
This is a key Quality of Life (QoL) differentiator. While most Markdown editors require manual 
syntax or toolbar clicks, Tessellum implements a custom Slash Command engine within 
CodeMirror 6. This allows for near -instant insertion of links, callouts, and code blocks, 
significantly reducing the friction usually associated with technical documentation. 
 
2.2 Technologies 
2.2.1 Rust 
2.2.1.1 Origin and Development  
Rust is a multi -paradigm, systems programming language focused on safety, speed, and 
concurrency. It originated as a personal project by Graydon Hoare at Mozilla Research in 2006 
and was officially announced in 2010. Since then, it has evolved through a community -driven 
model, becoming the foundation for high -performance software where memory safety is a 
critical requirement. 
2.2.1.2 Purpose and Industry Applications  
Rust was designed to address a long -standing trade-off in software engineering between the 
safety guarantees of managed languages like Java or Python and the performance of lower-level 
languages like C or C++. It achieves this through an ownership and borr owing system, which 
allows the compiler to guarantee memory safety and prevent data races at compile time, 
without relying on a garbage collector. 
Its adoption across the industry reflects this balance: 
• Infrastructure: Cloud providers such as AWS and Google use Rust in performance -
critical services. 
• Browsers: Mozilla used Rust to develop the Servo engine and parts of Firefox. 
• Operating Systems: Both the Linux kernel and Windows have begun incorporating Rust 
in selected components, with the goal of reducing memory-related vulnerabilities. 
2.2.1.3 Application in Project Tessellum  
In this project, Rust serves as the robust "Core" or backend of the application. It was selected 
over other systems languages for the following reasons: 
Commented [JC5]: @ENRIQUE JUAN DE ANDRES GALIANA

Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo | Jorge Carriles Ruiz 21 
 
• Memory Safety in File Operations:  Tessellum frequently interacts with the local 
filesystem to index thousands of files. Rust’s safety guarantees ensure that the 
application does not suffer from memory leaks or "segmentation faults" during these 
intensive operations. 
• High-Performance Indexing: The heavy lifting, including Markdown parsing, metadata 
extraction, and search query execution via Tantivy is handled in Rust, which avoids the 
overhead of a JavaScript runtime and allows the backend to remain responsive under 
significant indexing load. 
• Safe Concurrency: Using the Tokio runtime, Rust allows Tessellum to run background 
indexers and file watchers on multiple threads without the risk of data corruption, 
keeping the user interface fluid and responsive. 
2.2.1.4 Version Employed  
For this project, we have employed Rust 1.75+, utilizing the 2024 Edition features. This version 
ensures access to the most stable asynchronous paradigms and optimized compilation times 
necessary for a high-performance desktop environment. 
 
2.2.2 Tauri 
2.2.2.1 Origin and Development  
Tauri is an open -source polyglot framework used to build cross -platform desktop applications. 
It was created by the Tauri Programme  team and released to address the high resource 
consumption and security concerns associated with existing web-to-desktop frameworks. Unlike 
its predecessors, Tauri is built on a "security -first" philosophy and leverages the power of Rust 
for its core logic. 
2.2.2.2 Purpose and Industry Applications  
The primary purpose of Tauri is to enable developers to create desktop applications using any 
frontend framework (such as React, Vue, or Svelte) while keeping the binary size and memory 
usage extremely low. It achieves this by using the OS-native WebView  (e.g., WebView2 on 
Windows, WebKit on macOS) instead of bundling a full Chromium instance. 
Key industry applications include: 
• Security-Critical Apps: Due to its "Isolator" pattern and strict capability system, it is used 
for password managers and financial tools. 
• Performance-Driven Tools: Used for utilities that need to run in the background without 
consuming significant system RAM. 
===== PAGE 22 =====
Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs | Project Memory 
22 Jorge Carriles Ruiz | Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo 
 
• Cross-Platform Distribution: It allows for a single codebase to be deployed as a native 
executable on Windows, macOS, and Linux. 
2.2.2.3 Application in Project Tessellum  
Tauri serves as the "Bridge" and the "Shell" for Project Tessellum. It was chosen as a superior 
alternative to Electron for the following specific reasons: 
• Lightweight Footprint: Since Tessellum is designed to be a tool that users keep open all 
day, using Tauri ensures that the application consumes a fraction of the memory (RAM) 
compared to Chromium -based alternatives, leaving more resources for the user's 
primary tasks. 
• Inter-Process Communication (IPC): Tauri provides a high -speed asynchronous bridge 
between the React frontend and the Rust backend. In Tessellum, this is used to send 
search queries from the UI to the Tantivy engine and receive backlink data from the 
SQLx database almost instantaneously. 
• Native System Integration: Through Tauri’s plugin system, the project accesses native 
file dialogs, system notifications, and filesystem watchers without needing complex 
third-party wrappers. 
• Security: Tauri’s default configuration limits the frontend's access to the filesystem, 
ensuring that the user's "Vault" is only accessed through the strictly defined and audited 
Rust commands. 
2.2.2.4 Version Employed  
Project Tessellum utilizes Tauri 2.0 . This version was selected for its improved mobile -ready 
architecture (although currently limited to desktop scope) and its more robust plugin ecosystem, 
which simplifies the management of window states and filesystem permissions. 
 
2.2.3 React 
2.2.3.1 Origin and Development  
React is an open -source, front -end JavaScript library created by Jordan Walke , a software 
engineer at Meta (formerly Facebook). It was first deployed on Facebook's News Feed in 2011 
and released to the public in 2013. Since its inception, it has revolutionized web development 
by introducing a component -based architecture and a declarative approach to building user 
interfaces. 
===== PAGE 23 =====
Project Memory | Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs 
Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo | Jorge Carriles Ruiz 23 
 
2.2.3.2 Purpose and Industry Applications  
The primary purpose of React is to simplify the creation of complex, interactive UIs. It achieves 
this through two core innovations: 
• Component-Based Architecture: Interfaces are broken down into small, isolated pieces 
of code called "components," which can be composed to build complex applications. 
• Virtual DOM: React maintains a lightweight representation of the UI in memory. When 
the state of the application changes, React efficiently updates only the specific parts of 
the actual DOM that need to change, drastically improving performance. 
In the industry, React is the standard for high -performance Single Page Applications (SPAs), 
powering platforms such as Instagram, Airbnb, Netflix, and Discord. 
2.2.3.3 Application in Project Tessellum  
React serves as the "Visual Engine" of Project Tessellum. It was selected to manage the 
application's sophisticated frontend requirements: 
• Modular Interface: The UI is divided into distinct functional components —such as the 
File Explorer, the Markdown Editor (via CodeMirror), and the Graph View. This 
modularity allows for easier maintenance and testing of the 40+ user stories. 
• Reactive State Management:  In a knowledge management tool, the UI must update 
instantly when a file is renamed or a link is added. React’s state -driven nature ensures 
that the interface stays perfectly synchronized with the underlying data provided by the 
Rust backend. 
• Ecosystem Integration: By using React, Tessellum gains access to a vast ecosystem of 
high-quality libraries, such as Cytoscape.js for graph rendering and Zustand for 
lightweight state management, allowing for professional-grade features with a smaller 
development footprint. 
• Tauri Compatibility: React integrates seamlessly with the Tauri IPC bridge, allowing us 
to use modern web development workflows (hooks, effects) to handle asynchronous 
calls to the high-performance Rust core. 
2.2.3.4 Version Employed  
Project Tessellum utilizes React 18.3. This version is used for its stable support of "Concurrent 
Rendering," which ensures that the UI remains interactive even while rendering complex 
components like the interactive knowledge graph.

2.2.4 SQLite & SQLx 
2.2.4.1 Origin and Development  
SQLite was created by D. Richard Hipp in 2000 with the goal of allowing software to be operated 
without installing a database management system or requiring a database administrator. SQLx 
is a more recent, open-source library developed by the LaunchBadge team, specifically designed 
for the Rust ecosystem to provide an asynchronous, type-safe interface for SQL databases. 
2.2.4.2 Purpose and Industry Applications  
SQLite is not a client -server database engine; instead, it is a library that is linked into the 
application, making it an "embedded" database. It is the most widely deployed database engine 
in the world, used by: 
• Mobile Operating Systems:  Every Android and iOS device uses SQLite to store 
application data, messages, and settings. 
• Web Browsers: Chrome, Firefox, and Safari use it for history, cookies, and local storage. 
• Embedded Systems: Found in automotive systems, airplanes, and medical devices due 
to its high reliability and zero-configuration nature. 
SQLx serves as the modern "driver" that allows Rust applications to interact with SQLite. Its 
primary innovation is compile-time checked queries, which means the Rust compiler validates 
that your SQL syntax is correct and that the database schema matches your code before the 
program even runs. 
2.2.4.3 Application in Project Tessellum  
While Tessellum is a "local-first" Markdown application where the files are the source of truth, 
SQLite and SQLx provide the High-Performance Metadata Cache: 
• Relational Indexing:  Parsing 1,000 Markdown files to find "which notes link to the 
current one" is slow if done on the fly. Tessellum parses them once and stores the 
metadata (links, tags, file paths, and timestamps) in a structured SQLite database. 
• Graph Querying: SQLite’s relational power is used to perform complex recursive queries 
that identify clusters and connections within the Knowledge Graph, providing data to 
the Grafeo engine. 
• Asynchronous Reliability: By using SQLx, the application performs database reads and 
writes asynchronously. This ensures that when the indexer updates the cache, the user's 
typing experience in the editor remains completely fluid. 
• Portability: The database is stored as a single .db file within the application data folder, 
making it easy for users to back up their metadata without needing complex export 
tools. 
===== PAGE 25 =====
Project Memory | Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs 
Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo | Jorge Carriles Ruiz 25 
 
2.2.4.4 Version Employed  
Project Tessellum utilizes SQLx 0.8 with the SQLite driver enabled. This version supports the 
latest asynchronous features of the Tokio runtime and ensures maximum compatibility with the 
Rust 2024 edition. 
 
2.2.5 Tantivy 
2.2.5.1 Origin and Development  
Tantivy is a high -performance full-text search engine library written in Rust. It was created by 
Paul Masurel  and is heavily inspired by Apache Lucene. Since its inception, it has gained a 
reputation as the fastest search engine library in the Rust ecosystem, designed to provide the 
same power as industrial-grade tools like Elasticsearch but with a significantly smaller resource 
footprint. 
2.2.5.2 Purpose and Industry Applications  
The primary purpose of Tantivy is to provide low -latency, high-throughput search capabilities 
for structured and unstructured data. It utilizes an inverted index architecture and advanced 
compression techniques to store and query text efficiently. 
In the industry, Tantivy is employed in scenarios where performance and resource efficiency are 
paramount: 
• Search Infrastructure: Used by companies like Greptile and Quickwit to build cloud -
native search and log management platforms. 
• Embedded Applications: Ideal for desktop and mobile applications that require full-text 
search without the overhead of a Java Virtual Machine (JVM), which is required by 
Lucene-based tools. 
• Database Engines: It serves as the indexing backend for various modern databases that 
prioritize Rust’s safety and speed. 
2.2.5.3 Application in Project Tessellum  
Tantivy is the "Search Engine" of the project, working in tandem with the SQLite metadata cache. 
Its application in Tessellum includes: 
• Content Indexing: While SQLite stores relationships and tags, Tantivy indexes the actual 
body content of every Markdown file in the "Vault." This allows for nearly instant 
keyword retrieval regardless of the number of notes. 
• Advanced Query Support:  It enables users to perform complex searches, including 
Boolean logic (AND/OR/NOT), phrase matching, and prefix searches.

architecture. 
2.2.6.2 Purpose and Industry Applications  
The primary purpose of CodeMirror 6 is to provide a customizable text editor for the web that 
behaves like a professional IDE. It is designed to be extensible through a system of "Extensions" 
and "Facets," allowing developers to build exactly the features they need. 
It is a global industry standard, used in: 
• Developer Tools: Powering the inspector in browsers like Firefox and Chrome. 
• Note-Taking Apps: Integrated into industry leaders like Obsidian and Joplin.

2.2.7 Cytoscape.js 
2.2.7.1 Origin and Development  
Cytoscape.js is an open -source graph theory library for visualization and analysis, written in 
JavaScript. It was created by Max Franz at the University of Toronto and is maintained by the 
Cytoscape Consortium. While the original Cytoscape desktop software was built for biological 
research, the JavaScript version was engineered from the ground up to be a modern, web -
standard library capable of handling large-scale networks in the browser. 
2.2.7.2 Purpose and Industry Applications  
The primary purpose of Cytoscape.js is to enable developers to model and visualize relational 
data as networks of nodes and edges. It includes a sophisticated rendering engine that can 
handle thousands of elements while providing a rich API for graph theor y algorithms (such as 
finding the shortest path, detecting clusters, or calculating centrality). 
In the industry, it is widely utilized in fields that require structural analysis of data: 
• Bioinformatics: Visualizing molecular interaction networks and metabolic pathways. 
• Cybersecurity: Mapping network topologies to identify vulnerabilities or track the 
spread of threats. 
• Social Network Analysis:  Rendering complex relationships and identifying influential 
nodes within large communities. 
• Infrastructure Monitoring:  Visualizing cloud architectures and microservice 
dependencies. 
===== PAGE 28 =====
Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs | Project Memory 
28 Jorge Carriles Ruiz | Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo 
 
2.2.7.3 Application in Project Tessellum  
Cytoscape.js is the graphical engine that brings the project’s "Knowledge Graph" to life. It was 
selected for its performance-first architecture and its ability to handle dynamic data: 
• Network Rendering: It transforms the flat lists of links and files generated by the Grafeo 
engine into an interactive, visual canvas. 
• Interactivity: It provides the built-in logic for user interactions, such as dragging nodes, 
zooming into specific clusters, and selecting nodes to trigger file navigation in the editor. 
• Layout Algorithms: We utilize its force -directed layouts to automatically organize the 
notes in a way that reveals semantic clusters, ensuring that the "Vault" visualization is 
meaningful and not just a random cloud of points. 
• High Performance: Thanks to its optimized Canvas-based rendering, Cytoscape.js allows 
Tessellum users to explore vaults containing thousands of notes and links without 
experiencing frame-rate drops or UI lag. 
2.2.7.4 Version Employed

performance improvements for modern web browsers and integrates seamlessly with the 
React-based frontend of the application. 
 
2.2.8 Tailwind CSS 
2.2.8.1 Origin and Development  
Tailwind CSS is an open -source, utility -first CSS framework created by Adam Wathan  and 
released in 2017. Unlike traditional CSS frameworks (like Bootstrap) that provide pre -designed 
components, Tailwind provides low -level utility classes that allow developers to build 
completely custom designs directly in their HTML or component code. 
2.2.8.2 Purpose and Industry Applications  
The purpose of Tailwind is to speed up UI development while maintaining a small and highly 
optimized CSS bundle. It uses a "Just -in-Time" (JIT) compiler that scans the project files and 
generates only the CSS that is being used. 
It is currently one of the most popular styling tools in the industry, used by: 
• Modern Startups: For rapid prototyping and building unique brand identities without 
the "standardized" look of other frameworks. 
===== PAGE 29 =====
Project Memory | Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs 
Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo | Jorge Carriles Ruiz 29 
 
• Enterprise Platforms:  To maintain design system consistency across large -scale 
applications (e.g., Shopify, Netflix). 
2.2.8.3 Application in Project Tessellum  
Tailwind CSS is used to manage the aesthetic and layout of Tessellum. It was chosen to ensure 
the application remains "distraction-free" and performant: 
• Design Consistency: It provides a standardized scale for spacing, colors, and typography, 
ensuring that the interface feels professional and cohesive. 
• Optimized Performance:  By using Tailwind’s JIT compiler, the final CSS bundle for 
Tessellum is remarkably small, contributing to the fast load times required by the Tauri 
framework. 
• Responsive Layouts: It simplifies the creation of a flexible workspace, allowing the file 
explorer, editor, and graph view to resize elegantly across different screen dimensions. 
• Maintenance: Since styles are applied directly to React components, it eliminates the 
"dead CSS" problem and makes the UI much easier to refactor as the project's user 
stories evolve. 
2.2.8.4 Version Employed  
Project Tessellum utilizes Tailwind CSS 4.1 . This version includes the latest performance 
optimizations and a simplified configuration process that aligns with the modern Vite build 
toolchain used in the project. 
 
2.2.9 TypeScript 
2.2.9.1 Origin and Development  
TypeScript is a free and open -source high -level programming language developed and 
maintained by Microsoft. It was designed by Anders Hejlsberg (the lead architect of C#) and first 
released in 2012. It was created to address the difficulties of developing large-scale applications 
with JavaScript, which lacked static typing and sophisticated tooling at the time. 
2.2.9.2 Purpose and Industry Applications  
TypeScript is a syntactic superset of JavaScript , meaning that any valid JavaScript code is also 
valid TypeScript. Its primary purpose is to add static typing  to the language. This allows 
developers to define the "shape" of data (interfaces and types), enabling the compiler to catch 
errors during development rather than at runtime. 
In the industry, it has become the standard for professional web development: 
===== PAGE 30 =====
Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs | Project Memory 
30 Jorge Carriles Ruiz | Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo 
 
• Enterprise Applications: Used by companies like Slack, Airbnb, and Google to maintain 
massive codebases where refactoring JavaScript would be too risky. 
• Library Development: Most modern frontend libraries (including React and Tauri) are 
either written in or provide first -class support for TypeScript to improve the developer 
experience. 
• Full-stack Development: Used in both the frontend and the backend (Node.js/Deno) to 
share type definitions across the entire stack. 
2.2.9.3 Application in Project Tessellum  
TypeScript acts as the "Safety Net" for the frontend of Tessellum. It was selected to bring the 
same level of rigor to the React UI that Rust brings to the backend: 
• IPC Bridge Validation: One of the most critical parts of a Tauri app is the communication 
between the frontend and the Rust core. TypeScript allows us to define strict interfaces 
for the data being sent and received, ensuring that a "Search Result" from Rust perfectly 
matches what the React components expect. 
• Error Prevention:  By using static types, TypeScript identifies potential "null" or 
"undefined" errors during the coding phase. This is particularly important for the 40+ 
user stories, as it prevents regression bugs when adding new features to the editor or 
graph view. 
• Enhanced Tooling: It provides superior "IntelliSense" and auto-completion in VS Code, 
which significantly accelerated the development of complex logic, such as the 
interaction between the Markdown editor and the state management in Zustand. 
• Maintainability: As a solo developer project, TypeScript acts as a living documentation. 
Returning to a piece of code months later is easier because the types explicitly describe 
what every function and variable is supposed to do. 
2.2.9.4 Version Employed  
Project Tessellum utilizes TypeScript 5.6 . This version includes optimized build times and 
advanced type -checking features that align with the high -performance goals of the Vite 
toolchain. 
 
===== PAGE 31 =====
Project Memory | Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs 
Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo | Jorge Carriles Ruiz 31 
 
2.2.10 Semantic Markdown Extensions (Terminal 
Callouts) 
2.2.10.1 Origin and Development 
Originating in technical documentation frameworks (like MkDocs -Material or Docusaurus), 
these are specialized containers that extend standard Markdown to provide visual hierarchy and 
semantic meaning to specific blocks of text. 
2.2.10.2 Purpose and Importance 
Standard Markdown is often too limited for technical users. The importance of specialized 
extensions—like Terminal Callouts—is to provide a "prettier," high -fidelity representation of 
technical data (e.g., shell commands, compiler outputs) that standard "code fences" fail to 
distinguish effectively. 
2.2.10.3 Application in Project Tessellum 
This represents a functional novelty  aimed at the "Technical User" profile. Tessellum goes 
beyond standard rendering by including a custom -built engine for "Terminal Callouts." These 
blocks emulate a real terminal environment (including window controls and syntax highlighting 
CLI tools), making the documentation not only more aesthetic but also more readable for 
developers and system administrators.

3.2 Introduction 
The purpose of this project is the development of Tessellum, an advanced Personal Knowledge 
Management (PKM) tool, often referred to as a "Second Brain." The primary goal is to provide 
users—particularly those with a technical profile —with a digital environment where they can 
capture, organize, and connect their ideas seamlessly, transforming a collection of isolated notes 
into a structured, long-term knowledge network. 
3.2.1 Motivation and Needs Addressed 
Currently, most of the information we generate resides in the "cloud," which raises significant 
concerns regarding privacy, internet dependency, and a lack of control  over one's own data. 
Tessellum was created to address three critical needs identified in the current software 
ecosystem: 
• Data Sovereignty (Local -First): Many professionals handle sensitive information or 
intellectual property that they are unwilling to trust to third -party servers. Tessellum 
follows a local-first philosophy, meaning all data stays on the user's hardware, ensuring 
total privacy and permanent access. 
• Performance and Speed: Most modern tools are "heavy" and suffer from lag. Tessellum 
is designed to be lightweight and incredibly fast, ensuring that the software never slows 
down the user's train of thought. 
• Technical Specialization: There is a lack of tools that allow for high -fidelity technical 
documentation. Tessellum fills this gap by offering features tailored for developers, such 
as terminal callouts that render command-line outputs in a professional, aesthetic way. 
• Workflow Optimization (Quality of Life):  To maintain a "flow state," users need to 
format notes without distractions. Tessellum includes Quality of Life (QoL) features like 
slash commands , allowing users to trigger formatting and complex components 
instantly without leaving the keyboard. 
3.2.2 Applications and Justification 
Tessellum serves as an intellectual operations center. Its applications range from writing 
technical documentation and studying for complex certifications to planning large -scale 
projects. The project is justified by the demand for a high-performance tool  that does not 
compromise user privacy for the sake of functionality. 
3.2.3 Comparison 
When compared to popular market solutions, Tessellum offers several distinct advantages: 
• Vs. Notion: Unlike Notion, which is cloud -based and often suffers from loading times 
and connectivity issues, Tessellum is local-first. This makes it significantly faster and 
Commented [EG8]: En inglés

more reliable, as it does not rely on an internet connection or remote servers to 
function. It eliminates the "latency" that often interrupts the writing process in cloud -
heavy apps. 
• Vs. Obsidian:  While Obsidian is a strong local -first competitor, it often requires a 
complex web of third-party plugins to achieve a high-end technical workflow. Tessellum 
provides a "turnkey" experience  specifically for technical users. It includes built -in 
specialized features—like slash commands and terminal-style code rendering—that are 
optimized for performance out of the box, avoiding the bloat and stability issues often 
found in plugin-heavy environments. 
In summary, Tessellum is not just a text editor; it is a lightweight, fast, and secure infrastructure 
designed to make thinking as efficient and private as possible. 
 
3.3 Object 
The purpose of this project is to design and develop a personal knowledge management (PKM) 
application based on knowledge graphs and a local-first architecture. The primary objective is 
to provide an advanced note-taking tool that enables the relational organization of information, 
moving beyond the limitations of traditional hierarchical folder systems. 
The motivation for this project stems from a clear gap in current productivity software. Existing 
solutions are often polarized: some offer superior user experience and modern "Quality of Life" 
features but rely heavily on cloud storage and lack a true relational structure. Conversely, 
privacy-focused, graph -based alternatives often suffer from steep learning curves and less 
intuitive interfaces. 
This project is justified by the creation of a hybrid solution that bridges this gap. It combines the 
data sovereignty and relational power of a knowledge graph with the polished, user -friendly 
interface expected of modern document management tools. Compar ed to current market 
leaders, this application provides an environment where ease of use does not compromise the 
ability to visualize and manage complex connections between ideas. 
The applications for this project are diverse, proving particularly valuable in academic research, 
software development, and complex project management —fields where the ability to 
interconnect concepts is essential for deep learning and professional productivity. 
 
3.4 Description of the Current Situation 
The current landscape is characterized by a polarization in Personal Knowledge Management 
(PKM) tools. Users currently face a dilemma: choosing the ease of use and "Quality of Life" 
features of cloud-based platforms—which often compromise data sovereignty and lack complex

relational structures—or utilizing local, graph -based tools that offer steep learning curves and 
fragmented user experiences. 
This project is defined as an action of change designed to move from this scenario of isolated 
tools to an integrated ecosystem where data analysis power (graphs) and modern usability 
coexist within a private, local-first environment. 
The following starting elements have been identified for the execution of the project: 
• Human Resources: The project is carried out individually by the author, contributing 
expertise in software engineering, data structures, and UI/UX design. The primary 
constraint is the available development timeline, necessitating a focus on the core 
system architecture over non-essential secondary features. 
• Hardware Equipment:  A standard x86 -64 workstation and mobile devices for 
synchronization testing are available. No high -performance server infrastructure is 
required, as the software's local-first focus ensures that graph computation occurs on 
the client side. 
• Software Licenses and Starting Tools: Development relies on Open Source technologies 
to minimize licensing costs. Tools such as Visual Studio Code and modern frameworks 
for dynamic node visualization will be utilized. The final solution will be released under 
a license that promotes transparency and community use. 
• Data and Information:  As there is no pre -existing database, the project must include 
the design of import protocols for standard formats (such as Markdown) to facilitate 
user migration from current platforms.

3.5.3 Methods, Tools, Models, Metrics and 
Prototypes 
This section details the relationship of methods, prototypes, metrics, and tools used to develop 
the various calculations, estimations, and technical executions of the project.  
3.5.3.1 Methods 
• Scrum Framework : The primary Agile framework used to manage the development 
cycle through iterations (Sprints) of three weeks.  
• Local-First Architecture : An architectural method prioritizing local data storage and 
offline capability to ensure data sovereignty and low latency.  
• Asynchronous Programming: Implementation of the async/await paradigm using the 
Tokio runtime to handle concurrent file indexing without blocking the UI.  
3.5.3.2 Tools 
• Development Environments (IDEs): RustRover was used for high-performance backend 
engineering , and VS Code was used for frontend React and TypeScript development.  
• Project Management: Microsoft Project served as the primary tool for maintaining the 
Work Breakdown Structure (WBS) and calculating the critical path.  
• Testing and Profiling: Vitest and Testing Library were used for automated UI validation 
, while cargo flamegraph  was used for performance profiling to eliminate backend 
bottlenecks.  
===== PAGE 42 =====
Note Taking Application Based on Knowledge Graphs | Project Memory 
42 Jorge Carriles Ruiz | Escuela de Ingeniería Informática - Universidad de Oviedo 
 
• Version Control : Git and GitHub were used to maintain the codebase history and 
facilitate remote collaboration and backups.  
3.5.3.3 Models 
• Knowledge Graph Model: A relational information model based on nodes and semantic 
links used to visualize and analyze interconnected notes.  
• Weighted Complexity Model ($W_c$): A customized mathematical framework used to 
quantify the effort of user stories based on four variables: $V_{ui}$, $V_{db}$, 
$V_{graph}$, and $V_{sys}$.  
• Organization Breakdown Structure (OBS) : A functional model defining project roles 
such as Project Director, Architect, and Engineer.  
• User Personas: Five distinct user profiles (e.g., General User, Technical User, Knowledge 
Architect) used to model system requirements.  
3.5.3.4 Metrics 
• Rendering Latency: A technical performance target of $< 16ms$ to ensure fluid input 
and prevent UI lag.  
• Effort Estimation: Calculated using a Velocity Coefficient ($R = 0.85$), where 1 point of 
complexity is equivalent to approximately 51 minutes of development.  
• Binary Size: Continuous monitoring of build artifacts to ensure a lightweight footprint, 
targeting binaries under 10MB.  
3.5.3.5 Prototypes 
• Rapid Prototyping Phase : A dedicated 3 -week period used to test low -level logic and 
the Tauri IPC bridge before the main development sprints.  
• Interface Sketches: Structural UI mockups used to define the layout of the workspace, 
including the file explorer, editor, and graph view areas.

Project Redaction 
This section outlines the specific processes and standards utilized to guarantee the formal 
quality, technical accuracy, and structural consistency of the documentation. 
• Adherence to the UNE 157801 Standard : The report was drafted, structured, and 
presented following the criteria set forth in standard UNE 157801 (General criteria for

the development of Information Technology projects). This ensures methodological

README files and API references), ESLint and Prettier were used to maintain stylistic

3.6 Definitions and Abbreviations 
The following terms represent the core technical concepts and methodologies integrated into 
this project. This section provides a detailed description of each term to establish a unified 
conceptual framework and ensure technical clarity throughout the documentation. 
• Vault: A centralized digital repository in which the set of notes, references, 
relationships, and other knowledge artifacts managed by the user is stored, organized, 
and maintained. In this project, the vault constitutes the primary workspace and acts as 
a struc tural support for the creation, consultation, evolution, and interconnection of 
information. 
• Personal Knowledge Management (PKM):  A set of practices and methods oriented 
toward the capture, organization, connection, retrieval, and reuse of personal 
knowledge. Its purpose is to convert dispersed information into useful and actionable 
knowledge, facilitating processes of learning, analysis, reflection, and decision-making. 
In the proposed system, PKM is supported by mechanisms that allow for recording, 
contextualization, and meaningful linking of ideas. 
• Information Fragmentation: A state in which information remains fragmented, isolated, 
or distributed in disconnected units, hindering global understanding, efficient retrieval, 
and the identification of relevant relationships between contents. This fragmentation 
reduces the ability  to build knowledge in an integrated manner and limits the natural 
navigation between associated ideas. The project seeks to mitigate this issue through 
the creation of connections between notes and the use of tags, allowing information to 
be better structured, navigated more fluidly, and understood as part of an interrelated 
knowledge network rather than as independent elements. 
• Knowledge Graph:  A knowledge representation model based on nodes and 
relationships, in which each informative unit connects with others through explicit 
semantic links. This approach allows for the visualization and analysis of knowledge as 
a structured network, favoring the discovery of patterns, thematic affinities, 
dependencies, and connections that are not evident in linear or strictly hierarchical 
schemes. 
• Local-first Architecture:  An architectural approach in which data is prioritized for 
storage and processing on the user's local device. This model provides greater control 
over information, reduces dependency on external services, and allows the system to 
remain operational even in the absence of continuous connectivity. In the context of the 
project, this architecture reinforces privacy, availability, and autonomy in knowledge 
management. 
• Bi-directional Linking:  A reciprocal linking mechanism between two elements of 
information, such that the relationship can be recognized and traversed in both 
directions. This capability improves system navigability, enriches the context of each 
note, and favors a more organic e xploration of knowledge by allowing connections 
between ideas to emerge and remain explicit and accessible.

3.7 Initial Requirements 
The objective of this section is to define the external characteristics and essential functionalities 
of the product to be built, serving as a primary reference for final validation. These requirements 
ensure the system addresses the problem of information  fragmentation by providing a 
structured, relational environment for knowledge management.  
In accordance with the Scrum methodology utilized for Project Tessellum, these requirements 
are treated as the high-level Product Backlog. They are categorized into three primary domains:  
• Functional Requirements 
• Technical and Quality Constraints 
• Detailed Specifications 
The system is designed to transform static text into a dynamic knowledge network through the 
following core features: 
• The Technical Editor: A high-performance Markdown environment supporting real-time 
per-line rendering, specialized terminal callouts, and LaTeX-style scientific notation.  
• Knowledge Graph Infrastructure : Mechanisms for automated bi -directional link 
discovery and interactive visual mapping of note relationships via global and local graph 
views.  
• Extensible Ecosystem : A decoupled plugin API and theme engine allowing for the 
addition of custom system-level functionalities and visual adaptations.  
The application must adhere to strict non-functional requirements to ensure professional-grade 
reliability and usability: 
• Performance Benchmarks : Maintaining an input latency of < 16ms  and a graph 
navigation speed of 60 FPS for vaults with up to 2,000 notes.  
• Data Sovereignty: A strict "Local -First" architecture where user data is stored entirely 
offline in standard plain-text (.md) format to ensure portability and privacy.  
• Cross-Platform Stability: Native compatibility with Windows 10/11, macOS, and major 
Linux distributions via a lightweight hybrid architecture. 
 
A comprehensive breakdown of these requirements, including the full list of 46 individual User 
Stories, specific Acceptance Criteria, and the formal Definition of Done (DoD) , is provided in 
Chapter 5: System Specification.

3.8 Scope 
3.8.1 Introduction 
The purpose of this section is to establish the formal boundaries of the Tessellum project. To 
ensure a controlled and successful development cycle, it is critical to define not only the 
functionalities the system will implement but also the limitations and exclusions maintained 
throughout the analysis, design, and implementation phases. 
 
3.8.2 In-Scope: Functional and Technical 
Boundaries 
The development of Tessellum focuses on creating a high -performance, local-first knowledge 
management ecosystem. The following core capabilities are strictly within the scope of this 
project: 
3.8.2.1 Core Content Management 
• Markdown-Centric Editing:  Implementation of a robust, extensible editor based on 
CodeMirror. It supports Markdown syntax, YAML frontmatter (metadata), and asset 
embedding. 
• Local-First Data Ownership:  A system architecture where the "Vault" (the user's 
filesystem) remains the single source of truth. Data is stored locally in standard formats 
to ensure longevity and privacy. 
3.8.2.2 Automated Indexing and Search 
• Background Indexing Pipeline:  A Rust-based watcher and indexer that monitors 
filesystem changes and parses Markdown without interrupting the user experience. 
• Full-Text and Metadata Search:  Integration of the Tantivy library for high -speed 
indexing, allowing for advanced queries, tag-based filtering, and rapid content retrieval. 
3.8.2.3 Relational and Graph Visualization 
• Graph Projection (Grafeo): A specialized engine that transforms flat Markdown files and 
SQLite relationships into a navigable graph structure. 
• Interactive Graph View:  A visual interface powered by Cytoscape to explore 
connections, backlinks, and complex relationships between nodes.

3.8.2.4 Extensibility 
• Decoupled Plugin Runtime:  A framework allowing for the addition of new features —
such as UI components or backend logic —through a standardized, decoupled 
architecture. 
 
3.8.3 Out-of-Scope: Exclusions and Limitations 
To prevent scope creep and ensure project feasibility within the established timeframe, the 
following aspects are explicitly excluded: 
3.8.3.1 Connectivity and Sync 
• Cloud Synchronization: Native, built-in protocols (e.g., automated syncing with Dropbox 
or OneDrive) are excluded. Users are responsible for managing synchronization via 
external OS-level tools. 
• Real-Time Collaboration:  Multi-user features (such as CRDTs or Operational 
Transformation) for concurrent editing are not supported. The application is a single-
user environment. 
3.8.3.2 Platform Restrictions 
• Mobile and Web: Development is strictly limited to the Desktop environment via the 
Tauri framework. No iOS, Android, or browser -only versions will be developed in this 
phase. 
3.8.3.3 Format and AI Limitations 
• Proprietary Formats: The scope is bounded to Markdown and associated assets. Parsing 
or rendering complex formats like .docx, .pdf, or .odt is excluded. 
• Advanced AI Generation:  While the plugin architecture may allow for future 
integration, native Large Language Model (LLM)  integration or automated content 
generation is not a core requirement. 
 
3.9  Hypothesis and Restrictions 
In accordance with the UNE 157801 standard, this section establishes the fundamental premises 
and the technical or temporal limitations that condition the planning of Tessellum. These 
definitions are critical, as any variation in them will directly impact the project's schedule, cost, 
or scope.

3.9.1 Working Hypotheses 
The following premises are assumed to be true for the development of time and resource 
estimates: 
• Resource Availability: It is assumed that the lead developer will have a dedicated focus 
(according to the workload plan) during the execution period, without interruptions 
from external force majeure factors. 
• Technological Stability: It is presumed that the Rust ecosystem, the Tauri framework, 
and the core libraries ( Tantivy, CodeMirror) will maintain stable versions and will not 
undergo disruptive API changes that would require major refactoring during the project 
lifecycle. 
• Access to Documentation and Tools:  Uninterrupted access to official repositories, 
integrated development environments (IDEs), and the technical documentation 
necessary to implement the local-first architecture is taken for granted. 
• Test Hardware Compatibility: It is assumed that the current development equipment is 
representative of a standard desktop user environment, allowing for realistic 
performance validations. 
3.9.2 Project Constraints 
The following limits act as rigid boundaries that condition the execution: 
• Temporal Constraint (Deadline): The project must be submitted and defended by the 
non-extendable date established by the academic calendar. There is no margin for 
schedule extension beyond this milestone. 
• Economic Constraint (Budget): The budget is limited to personal resources and Open-
Source tools. The acquisition of proprietary software licenses or the hiring of paid cloud 
services is not contemplated. 
• Technical Scope Constraints: 
o Platform: Development is strictly limited to desktop operating systems 
(Windows, macOS, Linux) through the use of Tauri. 
o Data Format: The system will exclusively process and render files in Markdown 
format (.md) and YAML metadata. 
• Regulatory Compliance: The project must strictly adhere to the structure and formal 
quality requirements defined by the UNE 157801:2007 standard. 
• Data Architecture: The "Local-first" constraint prohibits the native storage of user data 
on external servers, requiring all indexing and persistence logic to occur on the local 
device.

3.10  Alternatives and Feasibility Study 
The development of Tessellum is predicated on the identification of a specific gap in the current 
Personal Knowledge Management (PKM) landscape: the need for a high-performance, local-first 
environment tailored specifically for technical users. To ensure the project’s technical viabi lity 
and unique value proposition, this section conducts a comprehensive feasibility study and 
competitive analysis. 
This study is divided into two primary dimensions: 
1. Functional Alternatives:  An evaluation of existing market leaders (Notion, Obsidian, 
Logseq, and VS Code) to determine where current workflows fail to meet the 
"lightweight" and "integrated technical QoL" requirements of the target demographic. 
2. Technical Feasibility:  A granular comparison of the architectural building blocks —
ranging from the desktop shell and backend logic to specialized engines for graph 
visualization, search, and math rendering. 
Each candidate is assessed against four critical project benchmarks: Performance Latency, Data 
Sovereignty, System Resource Footprint, and Extensibility. By analyzing the trade-offs of these 
industry-standard frameworks and libraries, we establish a data-driven justification for the final 
technology stack, ensuring that Tessellum avoids the "plugin bloat" and "high memory 
consumption" characteristic of its predecessors. 
The selected tech stack is defined in section 3.11 
 
3.10.1 Functional Alternatives 
Before defining a new technical architecture, it is essential to evaluate the current market 
leaders in Personal Knowledge Management (PKM). This sub -section analyzes how existing 
tools—ranging from cloud -native generalists to local -first specialized edito rs—handle the 
specific workflows of technical users. By identifying where these tools succeed and where they 
introduce friction (such as "input lag" or "vendor lock -in"), we define the functional 
requirements that Tessellum must fulfill. 
3.10.1.1 Notion 
3.10.1.1.1 Description 
Manufacturer: Notion Labs, Inc. (Founded by Ivan Zhao and Simon Last). 
Notion is a cloud -based, "all -in-one" workspace that pioneered the block -based editing 
experience. It is designed to replace multiple tools by integrating note -taking, complex 
databases, task management, and wikis into a single, cohesive interface. It oper ates on a 
software-as-a-service (SaaS) model, where all user data is stored on remote servers and 
accessed primarily via a web browser or a lightweight desktop wrapper.
